case_name: Fieldhouse v. Canada
parties: Between Rodney Fieldhouse, Jason Gallant, Dannie Fales, and Jeffrey Ewert, on behalf of themselves and all other prisoners of Kent Institution, located in the District of Agassiz in the Province of British Columbia, Plaintiffs (Appellants), and Her Majesty the Queen in Right of Canada and Robert Lusk, Warden of Kent Institution, and Dean Fox, Independent Chairperson of the Disciplinary Court at Kent Institution, Defendants (Respondents)
citation: "[1995] B.C.J. No. 975 DRS 95-14179 Vancouver Registry No. CA019247"
court: British Columbia Court of Appeal Vancouver, British Columbia Lambert, Gibbs and Hollinrake JJ.A.

Oral judgment: March 24, 1995.
(15 pp.)

Prisons -- Administration -- Regulation of drug use -- Civil rights -- Security of the person -- Law enforcement -- Serving sentence -- Urinalysis of prisoners -- Right to privacy -- Scope of right.

This was an appeal from a decision on an action for a declaration that section 54(b) of the *Corrections and Conditional Release Act* and sections 60 and 63 of the Corrections and Conditional Release Regulations were of no force and effect because they were contrary to sections 7, 8, 12 and 15 of the *Corrections and Conditional Release Act*. Three of the appellants were serving life sentences for first degree murder and the fourth a two year sentence for robbery. Submissions at trial were limited to sections 7 and 8. The impugned section authorized the imposition of a mandatory urinalysis program, one of a number of provisions dealing with search and seizure.

**HELD**: The appeal was dismissed. Given the nature and extent of the drug problems, the appellants could no more have a reasonable expectation of privacy in regard to urinalysis than was the case in another context in regard to a frisk search, the count and the wind. The judge had taken all the appropriate factors into account and reached the right conclusions in terms of the *Charter*. The impugned regulations keyed on security and safety. Given the limited privacy interest and minimal intrusion, the law was reasonable as were the provisions to carry it out.

Statutes, Regulations and Rules Cited:

*Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms*, 1982, ss. 7, 8, 12, 15, 24.

*Constitution Act*, 1982, s. 52(1).

*Corrections and Conditional Release Act*, S.C. 1992, c. 20, ss. 2, 3, 4, 46, 54(b).

Corrections and Conditional Release Regulations SOR/92-620, ss. 60, 63, 66.

Counsel for the Appellants: S. Pawliuk and P. Benning.

Counsel for the Respondents: D. Fitzsimmons and C. Haskell.

The judgment of the Court was delivered by Gibbs J.A.

GIBBS J.A. (orally):-- In November of 1993 the appellants sued the respondents by writ of summons and statement of claim for a declaration that s. 54(b) of the *Corrections and Conditional Release Act* S.C. 1992, c. 20 and s. 60 and s. 63 of the Corrections and Conditional Release Regulations SOR/92-620 are of no force and effect because contrary to ss. 7, 8, 12, and 15 of the *Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms*. The impugned statutory and regulatory provisions are an integral part of a legislative scheme of random urinalysis introduced into Canadian prisons in 1993 for narcotics detection purposes.

Three of the appellants are serving life sentences for first degree murder. The fourth, Jeffrey Ewart, is serving two years for robbery. All four are prisoners in the Kent Institution, a maximum security prison.

Mr. Justice Collver heard the trial of the action in June of 1994. Submissions before him were confined to alleged infringement of only ss. 7 and 8 Charter rights. In written reasons handed down in July of 1994 he dismissed the action.

In this appeal from Mr. Justice Collver's decision only one ground of appeal is put forward. It is stated in the appellant's factum as follows:

1. It is submitted that the learned trial Judge erred by holding that s. 54(b) of the *Corrections and Conditional Release Act* and s. 60 under "random selection" and s. 63 of the Corrections and Conditional Release Regulations do not violate ss. 7 and 8 of the *Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms*.

The prayer for relief in the statement of claim is similarly worded in that it asks for a declaration on the same grounds. In my opinion, the implication that it is the law which violates or infringes s. 7 and 8 *Charter* rights reflects a misunderstanding of the nature of those rights. The rights protected are personal to an individual and violation or infringement occurs by conduct. A law comes into the equation when the conduct is sought to be justified by law.

There will be rare cases, of which *Rodriguez* v. *Attorney General of British Columbia* (1993) 85 C.C.C. (3d) 15 (S.C.C.) is an example, where the Court will entertain a *Charter* challenge to a law without any antecedent conduct infringing rights. But those instances are grounded upon compelling reasons which are not present here.

Properly framed, the ground of appeal ought to allege error by the trial judge in failing to grant relief under s. 24 of the *Charter* on the ground that the impugned statutory and regulatory provisions are of no force and effect as provided by s. 52(1) of the *Charter* because they are inconsistent with ss. 7 and 8 of the *Charter*.

However, as the case has already consumed large amounts of time and money and as the evidence laid the foundation for ss. 7 and 8 submissions, and as the trial judge proceeded on the basis that he was trying and had tried a ss. 7 and 8 case, I would not, at this stage, dispose of the appeal on the ground of inartistic or inappropriate pleading.

The inmates of Kent Institution were introduced to the random urinalysis program by a notice from the warden stating:

The objective of these urinalysis programs is to substantially reduce substance abuse within the institution and the violence, both physical and psychological, which drugs and the drug trade cause in an institutional environment. The policy of the Correctional Service, stated by the Commissioner and by the Minister, is "Zero Tolerance" for intoxicants. This institution intends to follow that policy to the best of our ability and resources. (emphasis added)

At p. 4 of his reasons Mr. Justice Collver gave a brief description of the procedure followed or contemplated by way of implementation of the program:

The essentials of the process, described in the mentioned Warden's notice, can be briefly described.

Up to 5 percent (10 to 15 inmates) of Kent's population can be randomly selected for testing each month. Selection is done by computer, at Correctional Service of Canada ("CSC") headquarters. Samples must be obtained from the selected inmates within 30 days.

An inmate must comply within two hours of a demand being delivered -- his refusal being designated as a "serious charge" resulting in referral to the independent chairperson appointed pursuant to the Regulations. Positive results for intoxicants may also bring such a referral.

The notice mentions that convictions for either positive results or refusals can impact on transfer prospects, consideration for various forms of conditional release, and participation in programs involving contact with the community.

As to the procedure itself, a directive from the Commissioner of Corrections details the steps to be followed in collecting samples (in private) and subsequently analyzing them.

The word "intoxicants" in that description is to be understood as conveying the meaning attributed to it in s. 2 of the *Act*:

"intoxicant" means a substance that, if taken into the body, has the potential to impair or alter judgment, behaviour or the capacity to recognize reality or meet the ordinary demands of life, but does not include caffeine, nicotine or any authorized medication used in accordance with directions given by a staff member or a registered health care professional;

Notwithstanding that broad definition, the target of the program is, as the warden said, "substance abuse" and "the violence . . . which drugs and the drug trade cause in an institutional setting".

Section 54(b) of the *Act* authorizes the imposition
of a random urinalysis program:

54. Subject to section 56 and subsection 57(1), a staff member may demand that an inmate submit to urinalysis

(b) as part of a prescribed random selection urinalysis
program, conducted without individualized grounds on a periodic basis and in
accordance with any Commissioner's Directives that the regulations may provide
for; or

As is normally the case with statutory provisions, however, it is not appropriate to isolate and scrutinize a section or subsection out of the context in which it is found when seeking an understanding of the objectives sought to be achieved. Section 54(b) is found in a part of the Act entitled search and seizure starting at s. 46. That part addresses body cavity searches, frisk searches, non-intrusive searches, strip searches and urinalysis. All of the authorized forms of search and the persons who may be searched and the places where searches may be undertaken, are directed towards the detection of contraband which, in s. 2 of the Act, is defined to include intoxicants. Some of those authorized forms of search were the subject matter of the appeal in *Conway* v. *Attorney General of Canada* (1993) 83 C.C.C. (3d) 1 (S.C.C.) wherein La Forest, J. for the Court said, at p. 4:

Imprisonment necessarily entails surveillance, searching and scrutiny. A prison cell is expected to be exposed and to require observation. The frisk search, the count and the wind are all practices necessary in a penitentiary for the security of the institution, the public and indeed the prisoners themselves. A substantially reduced level of privacy is present in this setting and a prisoner thus cannot hold a reasonable expectation of privacy with respect to these practices. This conclusion is unaffected by the fact that the practices at times may be conducted by female guards. There being no reasonable expectation of privacy, s. 8 of the *Charter* is not called into play; nor is s. 7 implicated.

In my opinion the trial judge would have been justified in disposing of this case in the same manner and on the same grounds as in *Conway*. He accepted evidence led in the respondent's case, for example, that could not leave any doubt in the mind of an impartial listener that there is a very real necessity for a method of detecting narcotics trafficking and use in the Kent Institution for the "security of the institution, the public and even the prisoners themselves".

The scope of the problem is readily apparent from pages 6 to 9 of the Mr. Justice Collver's reasons. Some indication of it is evident from these descriptive observations: management problems associated with drug-related assaults, intimidation, underground (the market place) and overdoses leading to death; the power exerted by those who control the drug trade, the extent to which property and sexual favours are exchanged for drugs, tension both staff and inmates; beatings, requests for transfers to protective custody, pressure on visiting family members to import drugs, younger and weaker inmates being converted to mules for transporting drugs. And the litany goes on.

In my opinion, given the nature and extent of the problem, the appellants can no more have a reasonable expectation of privacy with respect to random selection urine sampling than they could have in Conway in relation to the frisk search, the count and the wind.

However, Mr. Justice Collver elected to couch his judgment in terms of the *Charter* sections relied upon by the appellants. In my respectful opinion in the course of doing so he took all of the appropriate factors into account, touching upon the relevant evidence from time to time, and reached the right conclusion.

In the discussion section of his reasons Mr. Justice Collver set the stage by reference to the purposes and principles of the correctional system:

Determination of the validity of the random urine testing program first requires a consideration of the purpose of the correctional system and the principles established to guide it, as expressed in the introductory provisions of the Act, which read:

3. The purpose of the federal correctional system is to contribute to the maintenance of a just, peaceful and safe society, by:

(a) carrying out sentences imposed by courts through
the safe and humane custody and supervision of offenders; and
(b) assisting the rehabilitation of offenders and their
reintegration into the community as law-abiding citizens through the provision
of programs in penitentiaries and in the community. (my emphasis)

S. 4 of the Act then sets out guiding principles, and those pertinent to this discussion are:

(a) that the protection of society be the paramount consideration in the corrections process;

(d) that the Service use the least restrictive measures consistent with the protection of the public, staff members and offenders;

(e) that offenders retain the rights and privileges of all members of society, except those rights and privileges that are necessarily removed or restricted as a consequence of the sentence;

I think that it is significant that the impugned s. 63 of the regulations carries forward the themes of security and safety:

63. (1) For the purposes of paragraph 54(b) of the Act, the Service may establish a random selection urinalysis program for the purpose of ensuring the security of the penitentiary and the safety of persons by deterring the use of and trafficking in intoxicants in the penitentiary.

(2) A random selection urinalysis program shall provide for samples to be provided by inmates whose names have been chosen by random selection from among the names of the entire inmate population of the penitentiary.(emphasis added)

Argument before this Court and, judging by the trial judge's reasons, in the court below followed the sequence of s. 7 considerations first, followed by s. 8. In my opinion where, as here, the principal alleged *Charter* breach is unreasonable search or seizure it is s. 8 which falls to be considered first. If there were no search or seizure there could not be a complaint under either section. If, by the application of the tests found in *R.* v. *Kokesh* (1990) 61 C.C.C. (3d) 207 (S.C.C.), the search or seizure is held not to be unreasonable it would seem to follow as a matter of logic that a consequent diminution of the liberty or security of the complainant would not be contrary to the principles of fundamental justice. The last sentence in the earlier quote from Conway appears to substantiate that conclusion: "There being no reasonable expectation of privacy, s. 8 of the *Charter* is not called into play; nor is s. 7 implicated."

The three *Kokesh* tests to be applied to determine
whether a search is or is not unreasonable are:

1. Is the search authorized by law?

2. Is the law reasonable?

3. Is the manner of carrying out the search reasonable?

The first does not require comment. The Act and the regulations authorize the search.

As to the second, the court is called upon to strike "a delicate balance.
. .between societal interests and individual rights": *Nova
Scotia Pharmaceutical Society* v. *The Queen*
[1992] 2 S.C.R. 606 at 642; and *Cunningham* v.
*The Queen* (1993) 80 C.C.C. (3d) 492 (S.C.C.)
at 499. Both of those are s. 7 cases but the weighing process appears to be
the same as in s. 8. The trial judge understood the requirement for a balancing
exercise. He specifically referred to the submissions of counsel on the point
at p. 11 of his reasons. And at p. 15, after referring to the means by which
drugs are introduced into the institution he described what he called the "uneasy
choice" facing the prison authorities:

That presents CSC with an uneasy choice -- dissuading drug smugglers by intensifying interdiction efforts, or deterring importation by concentrating on inmate users. The former is unacceptable since it would necessarily entail intrusive body searches and x-rays. The latter has spawned this lawsuit.

Given the magnitude and pervasiveness of the problem and the minimal intrusion into the already limited privacy expectation of the inmates I have no difficulty in concluding that the balance falls heavily in favour of the societal interest. It follows that in my opinion the law is reasonable. In reaching that opinion I have taken into account the allegations about over breadth and unfairness associated with the random aspect, and about the absence of empirical data to prove that the objectives will be achieved.

The third *Kokesh* test requires consideration of the manner of carrying out the program. That subject is dealt with in part by s. 66 of the regulations and in part by Commissioner's Directive No. 572 and guidelines to the Directive, all emanating from the Correctional Service of Canada. Much was made in argument of the fact that neither the Directive nor the guidelines have the force of law but that seems to me to be irrelevant. Together the regulations, the Directive and the guidelines prescribe the manner of carrying out the program. Mr. Justice Collver found that the requirements were "meticulously followed at Kent Institution". The evidence supporting that conclusion, which is not disputed, seems to me to be a sufficient account of the manner of carrying out to form the basis for a finding as to reasonableness whether the Directive and the guidelines can or cannot properly be described as law.

Mr. Justice Collver summed up his view of the reasonable manner test as applied to the facts of this case in these words at p. 17 of his reasons:

In any event, the evidence establishes that seizures are carried out in a reasonable manner. Although urination on demand is unusual, the two hour time period given for compliance addresses that. Although urination in the presence of an observer (of the same sex) is also unusual, the need to ensure that the sample is not tampered with seems obvious, and the fact of observation does not, by itself, make the collection process unreasonable.

I agree with that conclusion. There is nothing useful which I can add to it.

As to s. 7 of the *Charter*, as I mentioned earlier, it seems to me that when a law is found to meet the *Kokesh* tests for purposes of a search or seizure under s. 8 it follows that the same law will meet the fundamental justice requirement of s. 7 to justify a deprivation or diminution of the right to liberty and security of the person. I take that proposition from the passage earlier quoted from *Conway*.

If, notwithstanding what I have said about the connection which appears to me to exist between ss. 7 and 8 of the *Charter* in search and seizure cases, s. 7 requires a kind of stand alone consideration I am content that my analysis of the s. 8 factors as are applied to the evidence in this case be applied also to s. 7. I am satisfied that the result would be the same. There may be one additional factor and that is a purpose test stated at p. 68 of *Rodriguez* in these words:

Where deprivation of the right in question does little or nothing to enhance the state's interest (whatever it may be), it seems to me that a breach of fundamental justice will be made out, as the individual's rights will have been deprived for no valid purpose.

It cannot, in my view, be seriously contended otherwise than that the prospect of advancing the state's interest in this case, given the nature of that interest, constitutes a valid purpose which justifies the infringement upon s. 7 rights.

The trial judge did not distinguish between s. 7 and s. 8 considerations in his discussion section or in his conclusions. As I have explained, I see no need to either. He ended his judgment with this summary paragraph:

Both fulfilment of its mandate and application of the statutory principles which guide its pursuit of that mandate justify the Correctional Service of Canada conducting random urine testing at Kent Institution. Moreover, as a deterrent to prison drug use and the violence associated with it, random urine testing constitutes neither an unreasonable diminution of inmates' liberty nor an unreasonable invasion of privacy or the integrity of their persons. I find no infringement of Sections 7 or 8 of the Charter.

I agree with that conclusion. Accordingly, I would dismiss the appeal.

GIBBS J.A.

**LAMBERT J.A.**:-- Mr. Justice Gibbs has expressed the views that underline his reservations about the process by which this petition was brought and has come before the Court. Notwithstanding those views and reservations Mr. Justice Gibbs has dealt with the substance of the petition and the appeal. I agree with Mr. Justice Gibbs' disposition of the substance of the appeal and I do so for the reasons he has given. I wish to express no views at all about the question about how this petition was framed or the propriety of bringing it before the Court in that way.

**HOLLINRAKE J.A.**:-- I agree with what both Mr. Justice Gibbs and Mr. Justice Lambert have said and I too would dismiss the appeal.

*Prisons -- Administration -- Regulation of drug use -- Civil rights -- Security of the person -- Law enforcement -- Serving sentence -- Urinalysis of prisoners -- Right to privacy -- Scope of right.*

This was an appeal from a decision on an action for a declaration that section 54(b) of the *Corrections and Conditional Release Act* and sections 60 and 63 of the Corrections and Conditional Release Regulations were of no force and effect because they were contrary to sections 7, 8, 12 and 15 of the *Corrections and Conditional Release Act*. Three of the appellants were serving life sentences for first degree murder and the fourth a two year sentence for robbery. Submissions at trial were limited to sections 7 and 8. The impugned section authorized the imposition of a mandatory urinalysis program, one of a number of provisions dealing with search and seizure.

**HELD**: The appeal was dismissed. Given the nature and extent of the drug problems, the appellants could no more have a reasonable expectation of privacy in regard to urinalysis than was the case in another context in regard to a frisk search, the count and the wind. The judge had taken all the appropriate factors into account and reached the right conclusions in terms of the *Charter*. The impugned regulations keyed on security and safety. Given the limited privacy interest and minimal intrusion, the law was reasonable as were the provisions to carry it out.

Statutes, Regulations and Rules Cited:

*Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms*, 1982, ss. 7, 8, 12, 15, 24.

*Constitution Act*, 1982, s. 52(1).

*Corrections and Conditional Release Act*, S.C. 1992, c. 20, ss. 2, 3, 4, 46, 54(b).

Corrections and Conditional Release Regulations SOR/92-620, ss. 60, 63, 66.

Counsel for the Appellants: S. Pawliuk and P. Benning.

Counsel for the Respondents: D. Fitzsimmons and C. Haskell.

The judgment of the Court was delivered by Gibbs J.A.

GIBBS J.A. (orally):-- In November of 1993 the appellants sued the respondents by writ of summons and statement of claim for a declaration that s. 54(b) of the *Corrections and Conditional Release Act* S.C. 1992, c. 20 and s. 60 and s. 63 of the Corrections and Conditional Release Regulations SOR/92-620 are of no force and effect because contrary to ss. 7, 8, 12, and 15 of the *Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms*. In this appeal from Mr. Justice Collver's decision only one ground of appeal is put forward. It is stated in the appellant's factum as follows:

(3d) 15 (S.C.C.) is an example, where the Court will entertain a *Charter* challenge to a law without any antecedent conduct infringing rights. But those instances are grounded upon compelling reasons which are not present here.

Properly framed, the ground of appeal ought to allege error by the trial judge in failing to grant relief under s. 24 of the *Charter* on the ground that the impugned statutory and regulatory provisions are of no force and effect as provided by s. 52(1) of the *Charter* because they are inconsistent with ss. 7 and 8 of the *Charter*.

However, as the case has already consumed large amounts of time and money and as the evidence laid the foundation for ss. 7 and 8 submissions, and as the trial judge proceeded on the basis that he was trying and had tried a ss. 7 and 8 case, I would not, at this stage, dispose of the appeal on the ground of inartistic or inappropriate pleading.

The inmates of Kent Institution were introduced to the random urinalysis program by a notice from the warden stating:

The objective of these urinalysis programs is to substantially reduce substance abuse within the institution and the violence, both physical and psychological, which drugs and the drug trade cause in an institutional environment. The policy of the Correctional Service, stated by the Commissioner and by the Minister, is "Zero Tolerance" for intoxicants. This institution intends to follow that policy to the best of our ability and resources. (emphasis added)

At p. 4 of his reasons Mr. Justice Collver gave a brief description of the procedure followed or contemplated by way of implementation of the program:

The essentials of the process, described in the mentioned Warden's notice, can be briefly described.

Up to 5 percent (10 to 15 inmates) of Kent's population can be randomly selected for testing each month. Selection is done by computer, at Correctional Service of Canada ("CSC") headquarters. Samples must be obtained from the selected inmates within 30 days.

An inmate must comply within two hours of a demand being delivered -- his refusal being designated as a "serious charge" resulting in referral to the independent chairperson appointed pursuant to the Regulations. Positive results for intoxicants may also bring such a referral.

The notice mentions that convictions for either positive results or refusals can impact on transfer prospects, consideration for various forms of conditional release, and participation in programs involving contact with the community.

As to the procedure itself, a directive from the Commissioner of Corrections details the steps to be followed in collecting samples (in private) and subsequently analyzing them.

The word "intoxicants" in that description is to be understood as conveying the meaning attributed to it in s. 2 of the *Act*:

"intoxicant" means a substance that, if taken into the body, has the potential to impair or alter judgment, behaviour or the capacity to recognize reality or meet the ordinary demands of life, but does not include caffeine, nicotine or any authorized medication used in accordance with directions given by a staff member or a registered health care professional;

Notwithstanding that broad definition, the target of the program is, as the warden said, "substance abuse" and "the violence . . . which drugs and the drug trade cause in an institutional setting".

Section 54(b) of the *Act* authorizes the imposition
of a random urinalysis program:

54. Subject to section 56 and subsection 57(1), a staff member may demand that an inmate submit to urinalysis

(b) as part of a prescribed random selection urinalysis
program, conducted without individualized grounds on a periodic basis and in
accordance with any Commissioner's Directives that the regulations may provide
for; or

As is normally the case with statutory provisions, however, it is not appropriate to isolate and scrutinize a section or subsection out of the context in which it is found when seeking an understanding of the objectives sought to be achieved. Section 54(b) is found in a part of the Act entitled search and seizure starting at s. 46. That part addresses body cavity searches, frisk searches, non-intrusive searches, strip searches and urinalysis. All of the authorized forms of search and the persons who may be searched and the places where searches may be undertaken, are directed towards the detection of contraband which, in s. 2 of the Act, is defined to include intoxicants. Some of those authorized forms of search were the subject matter of the appeal in *Conway* v. *Attorney General of Canada* (1993) 83 C.C.C. (3d) 1 (S.C.C.) wherein La Forest, J. for the Court said, at p. 4:

The frisk search, the count and the wind are all practices necessary in a penitentiary for the security of the institution, the public and indeed the prisoners themselves. This conclusion is unaffected by the fact that the practices at times may be conducted by female guards. There being no reasonable expectation of privacy, s. 8 of the *Charter* is not called into play; nor is s. 7 implicated.

In my opinion the trial judge would have been justified in disposing of this case in the same manner and on the same grounds as in *Conway*. He accepted evidence led in the respondent's case, for example, that could not leave any doubt in the mind of an impartial listener that there is a very real necessity for a method of detecting narcotics trafficking and use in the Kent Institution for the "security of the institution, the public and even the prisoners themselves".

The scope of the problem is readily apparent from pages 6 to 9 of the Mr. Justice Collver's reasons. Some indication of it is evident from these descriptive observations: management problems associated with drug-related assaults, intimidation, underground (the market place) and overdoses leading to death; the power exerted by those who control the drug trade, the extent to which property and sexual favours are exchanged for drugs, tension both staff and inmates; beatings, requests for transfers to protective custody, pressure on visiting family members to import drugs, younger and weaker inmates being converted to mules for transporting drugs. And the litany goes on.

In my opinion, given the nature and extent of the problem, the appellants can no more have a reasonable expectation of privacy with respect to random selection urine sampling than they could have in Conway in relation to the frisk search, the count and the wind.

However, Mr. Justice Collver elected to couch his judgment in terms of the *Charter* sections relied upon by the appellants. In my respectful opinion in the course of doing so he took all of the appropriate factors into account, touching upon the relevant evidence from time to time, and reached the right conclusion.

In the discussion section of his reasons Mr. Justice Collver set the stage by reference to the purposes and principles of the correctional system:

Determination of the validity of the random urine testing program first requires a consideration of the purpose of the correctional system and the principles established to guide it, as expressed in the introductory provisions of the Act, which read:

3. The purpose of the federal correctional system is to contribute to the maintenance of a just, peaceful and safe society, by:

(a) carrying out sentences imposed by courts through
the safe and humane custody and supervision of offenders; and
(b) assisting the rehabilitation of offenders and their
reintegration into the community as law-abiding citizens through the provision
of programs in penitentiaries and in the community. (my emphasis)

S. 4 of the Act then sets out guiding principles, and those pertinent to this discussion are:

(a) that the protection of society be the paramount consideration in the corrections process;

(d) that the Service use the least restrictive measures consistent with the protection of the public, staff members and offenders;

(e) that offenders retain the rights and privileges of all members of society, except those rights and privileges that are necessarily removed or restricted as a consequence of the sentence;

I think that it is significant that the impugned s. 63 of the regulations carries forward the themes of security and safety:

63. (1) For the purposes of paragraph 54(b) of the Act, the Service may establish a random selection urinalysis program for the purpose of ensuring the security of the penitentiary and the safety of persons by deterring the use of and trafficking in intoxicants in the penitentiary.

(2) A random selection urinalysis program shall provide for samples to be provided by inmates whose names have been chosen by random selection from among the names of the entire inmate population of the penitentiary.(emphasis added)

Argument before this Court and, judging by the trial judge's reasons, in the court below followed the sequence of s. 7 considerations first, followed by s. 8. In my opinion where, as here, the principal alleged *Charter* breach is unreasonable search or seizure it is s. 8 which falls to be considered first. If there were no search or seizure there could not be a complaint under either section. If, by the application of the tests found in *R.* v. *Kokesh* (1990) 61 C.C.C. (3d) 207 (S.C.C.), the search or seizure is held not to be unreasonable it would seem to follow as a matter of logic that a consequent diminution of the liberty or security of the complainant would not be contrary to the principles of fundamental justice. The last sentence in the earlier quote from Conway appears to substantiate that conclusion: "There being no reasonable expectation of privacy, s. 8 of the *Charter* is not called into play; nor is s. 7 implicated."

The three *Kokesh* tests to be applied to determine
whether a search is or is not unreasonable are:

1. Is the search authorized by law?

2. Is the law reasonable?

3. Is the manner of carrying out the search reasonable?

The first does not require comment. The Act and the regulations authorize the search.

As to the second, the court is called upon to strike "a delicate balance.
. .between societal interests and individual rights": *Nova
Scotia Pharmaceutical Society* v. *The Queen*
[1992] 2 S.C.R. 606 at 642; and *Cunningham* v.
*The Queen* (1993) 80 C.C.C. (3d) 492 (S.C.C.)
at 499. Both of those are s. 7 cases but the weighing process appears to be
the same as in s. 8. The trial judge understood the requirement for a balancing
exercise. He specifically referred to the submissions of counsel on the point
at p. 11 of his reasons. And at p. 15, after referring to the means by which
drugs are introduced into the institution he described what he called the "uneasy
choice" facing the prison authorities:

That presents CSC with an uneasy choice -- dissuading drug smugglers by intensifying interdiction efforts, or deterring importation by concentrating on inmate users. The former is unacceptable since it would necessarily entail intrusive body searches and x-rays. The latter has spawned this lawsuit.

Given the magnitude and pervasiveness of the problem and the minimal intrusion into the already limited privacy expectation of the inmates I have no difficulty in concluding that the balance falls heavily in favour of the societal interest. It follows that in my opinion the law is reasonable. In reaching that opinion I have taken into account the allegations about over breadth and unfairness associated with the random aspect, and about the absence of empirical data to prove that the objectives will be achieved.

The third *Kokesh* test requires consideration of the manner of carrying out the program. That subject is dealt with in part by s. 66 of the regulations and in part by Commissioner's Directive No. 572 and guidelines to the Directive, all emanating from the Correctional Service of Canada. Much was made in argument of the fact that neither the Directive nor the guidelines have the force of law but that seems to me to be irrelevant. Together the regulations, the Directive and the guidelines prescribe the manner of carrying out the program. Mr. Justice Collver found that the requirements were "meticulously followed at Kent Institution". The evidence supporting that conclusion, which is not disputed, seems to me to be a sufficient account of the manner of carrying out to form the basis for a finding as to reasonableness whether the Directive and the guidelines can or cannot properly be described as law.

Mr. Justice Collver summed up his view of the reasonable manner test as applied to the facts of this case in these words at p. 17 of his reasons:

In any event, the evidence establishes that seizures are carried out in a reasonable manner. Although urination on demand is unusual, the two hour time period given for compliance addresses that. Although urination in the presence of an observer (of the same sex) is also unusual, the need to ensure that the sample is not tampered with seems obvious, and the fact of observation does not, by itself, make the collection process unreasonable.

I agree with that conclusion. There is nothing useful which I can add to it.

As to s. 7 of the *Charter*, as I mentioned earlier, it seems to me that when a law is found to meet the *Kokesh* tests for purposes of a search or seizure under s. 8 it follows that the same law will meet the fundamental justice requirement of s. 7 to justify a deprivation or diminution of the right to liberty and security of the person. I take that proposition from the passage earlier quoted from *Conway*.

If, notwithstanding what I have said about the connection which appears to me to exist between ss. 7 and 8 of the *Charter* in search and seizure cases, s. 7 requires a kind of stand alone consideration I am content that my analysis of the s. 8 factors as are applied to the evidence in this case be applied also to s. 7. I am satisfied that the result would be the same. There may be one additional factor and that is a purpose test stated at p. 68 of *Rodriguez* in these words:

Where deprivation of the right in question does little or nothing to enhance the state's interest (whatever it may be), it seems to me that a breach of fundamental justice will be made out, as the individual's rights will have been deprived for no valid purpose.

It cannot, in my view, be seriously contended otherwise than that the prospect of advancing the state's interest in this case, given the nature of that interest, constitutes a valid purpose which justifies the infringement upon s. 7 rights.

The trial judge did not distinguish between s. 7 and s. 8 considerations in his discussion section or in his conclusions. As I have explained, I see no need to either. He ended his judgment with this summary paragraph:

Both fulfilment of its mandate and application of the statutory principles which guide its pursuit of that mandate justify the Correctional Service of Canada conducting random urine testing at Kent Institution. Moreover, as a deterrent to prison drug use and the violence associated with it, random urine testing constitutes neither an unreasonable diminution of inmates' liberty nor an unreasonable invasion of privacy or the integrity of their persons. I find no infringement of Sections 7 or 8 of the Charter.

I agree with that conclusion. Accordingly, I would dismiss the appeal.

GIBBS J.A.

**LAMBERT J.A.**:-- Mr. Justice Gibbs has expressed the views that underline his reservations about the process by which this petition was brought and has come before the Court. Notwithstanding those views and reservations Mr. Justice Gibbs has dealt with the substance of the petition and the appeal. I agree with Mr. Justice Gibbs' disposition of the substance of the appeal and I do so for the reasons he has given. I wish to express no views at all about the question about how this petition was framed or the propriety of bringing it before the Court in that way.

**HOLLINRAKE J.A.**:-- I agree with what both Mr. Justice Gibbs and Mr. Justice Lambert have said and I too would dismiss the appeal.

**HELD**: The appeal was dismissed. Given the nature and extent of the drug problems, the appellants could no more have a reasonable expectation of privacy in regard to urinalysis than was the case in another context in regard to a frisk search, the count and the wind. The judge had taken all the appropriate factors into account and reached the right conclusions in terms of the *Charter*. The impugned regulations keyed on security and safety. Given the limited privacy interest and minimal intrusion, the law was reasonable as were the provisions to carry it out.

Statutes, Regulations and Rules Cited:

*Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms*, 1982, ss. 7, 8, 12, 15, 24.

*Constitution Act*, 1982, s. 52(1).

*Corrections and Conditional Release Act*, S.C. 1992, c. 20, ss. 2, 3, 4, 46, 54(b).

Corrections and Conditional Release Regulations SOR/92-620, ss. 60, 63, 66.

Counsel for the Appellants: S. Pawliuk and P. Benning.

Counsel for the Respondents: D. Fitzsimmons and C. Haskell.

The judgment of the Court was delivered by Gibbs J.A.

GIBBS J.A. (orally):-- In November of 1993 the appellants sued the respondents by writ of summons and statement of claim for a declaration that s. 54(b) of the *Corrections and Conditional Release Act* S.C. 1992, c. 20 and s. 60 and s. 63 of the Corrections and Conditional Release Regulations SOR/92-620 are of no force and effect because contrary to ss. 7, 8, 12, and 15 of the *Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms*. The impugned statutory and regulatory provisions are an integral part of a legislative scheme of random urinalysis introduced into Canadian prisons in 1993 for narcotics detection purposes.

Three of the appellants are serving life sentences for first degree murder. The fourth, Jeffrey Ewart, is serving two years for robbery. All four are prisoners in the Kent Institution, a maximum security prison.

Mr. Justice Collver heard the trial of the action in June of 1994. Submissions before him were confined to alleged infringement of only ss. 7 and 8 Charter rights. In written reasons handed down in July of 1994 he dismissed the action.

In this appeal from Mr. Justice Collver's decision only one ground of appeal is put forward. It is stated in the appellant's factum as follows:

1. It is submitted that the learned trial Judge erred by holding that s. 54(b) of the *Corrections and Conditional Release Act* and s. 60 under "random selection" and s. 63 of the Corrections and Conditional Release Regulations do not violate ss. 7 and 8 of the *Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms*.

The prayer for relief in the statement of claim is similarly worded in that it asks for a declaration on the same grounds. In my opinion, the implication that it is the law which violates or infringes s. 7 and 8 *Charter* rights reflects a misunderstanding of the nature of those rights. The rights protected are personal to an individual and violation or infringement occurs by conduct. A law comes into the equation when the conduct is sought to be justified by law.

There will be rare cases, of which *Rodriguez* v. *Attorney General of British Columbia* (1993) 85 C.C.C. (3d) 15 (S.C.C.) is an example, where the Court will entertain a *Charter* challenge to a law without any antecedent conduct infringing rights. But those instances are grounded upon compelling reasons which are not present here.

Properly framed, the ground of appeal ought to allege error by the trial judge in failing to grant relief under s. 24 of the *Charter* on the ground that the impugned statutory and regulatory provisions are of no force and effect as provided by s. 52(1) of the *Charter* because they are inconsistent with ss. 7 and 8 of the *Charter*.

However, as the case has already consumed large amounts of time and money and as the evidence laid the foundation for ss. 7 and 8 submissions, and as the trial judge proceeded on the basis that he was trying and had tried a ss. 7 and 8 case, I would not, at this stage, dispose of the appeal on the ground of inartistic or inappropriate pleading.

The inmates of Kent Institution were introduced to the random urinalysis program by a notice from the warden stating:

The objective of these urinalysis programs is to substantially reduce substance abuse within the institution and the violence, both physical and psychological, which drugs and the drug trade cause in an institutional environment. The policy of the Correctional Service, stated by the Commissioner and by the Minister, is "Zero Tolerance" for intoxicants. This institution intends to follow that policy to the best of our ability and resources. (emphasis added)

At p. 4 of his reasons Mr. Justice Collver gave a brief description of the procedure followed or contemplated by way of implementation of the program:

The essentials of the process, described in the mentioned Warden's notice, can be briefly described.

Up to 5 percent (10 to 15 inmates) of Kent's population can be randomly selected for testing each month. Selection is done by computer, at Correctional Service of Canada ("CSC") headquarters. Samples must be obtained from the selected inmates within 30 days.

An inmate must comply within two hours of a demand being delivered -- his refusal being designated as a "serious charge" resulting in referral to the independent chairperson appointed pursuant to the Regulations. Positive results for intoxicants may also bring such a referral.

The notice mentions that convictions for either positive results or refusals can impact on transfer prospects, consideration for various forms of conditional release, and participation in programs involving contact with the community.

As to the procedure itself, a directive from the Commissioner of Corrections details the steps to be followed in collecting samples (in private) and subsequently analyzing them.

The word "intoxicants" in that description is to be understood as conveying the meaning attributed to it in s. 2 of the *Act*:

"intoxicant" means a substance that, if taken into the body, has the potential to impair or alter judgment, behaviour or the capacity to recognize reality or meet the ordinary demands of life, but does not include caffeine, nicotine or any authorized medication used in accordance with directions given by a staff member or a registered health care professional;

Notwithstanding that broad definition, the target of the program is, as the warden said, "substance abuse" and "the violence . . . which drugs and the drug trade cause in an institutional setting".

Section 54(b) of the *Act* authorizes the imposition
of a random urinalysis program:

54. Subject to section 56 and subsection 57(1), a staff member may demand that an inmate submit to urinalysis

(b) as part of a prescribed random selection urinalysis
program, conducted without individualized grounds on a periodic basis and in
accordance with any Commissioner's Directives that the regulations may provide
for; or

As is normally the case with statutory provisions, however, it is not appropriate to isolate and scrutinize a section or subsection out of the context in which it is found when seeking an understanding of the objectives sought to be achieved. Section 54(b) is found in a part of the Act entitled search and seizure starting at s. 46. That part addresses body cavity searches, frisk searches, non-intrusive searches, strip searches and urinalysis. All of the authorized forms of search and the persons who may be searched and the places where searches may be undertaken, are directed towards the detection of contraband which, in s. 2 of the Act, is defined to include intoxicants. Some of those authorized forms of search were the subject matter of the appeal in *Conway* v. *Attorney General of Canada* (1993) 83 C.C.C. (3d) 1 (S.C.C.) wherein La Forest, J. for the Court said, at p. 4:

Imprisonment necessarily entails surveillance, searching and scrutiny. A prison cell is expected to be exposed and to require observation. The frisk search, the count and the wind are all practices necessary in a penitentiary for the security of the institution, the public and indeed the prisoners themselves. A substantially reduced level of privacy is present in this setting and a prisoner thus cannot hold a reasonable expectation of privacy with respect to these practices. This conclusion is unaffected by the fact that the practices at times may be conducted by female guards. There being no reasonable expectation of privacy, s. 8 of the *Charter* is not called into play; nor is s. 7 implicated.

In my opinion the trial judge would have been justified in disposing of this case in the same manner and on the same grounds as in *Conway*. He accepted evidence led in the respondent's case, for example, that could not leave any doubt in the mind of an impartial listener that there is a very real necessity for a method of detecting narcotics trafficking and use in the Kent Institution for the "security of the institution, the public and even the prisoners themselves".

The scope of the problem is readily apparent from pages 6 to 9 of the Mr. Justice Collver's reasons. Some indication of it is evident from these descriptive observations: management problems associated with drug-related assaults, intimidation, underground (the market place) and overdoses leading to death; the power exerted by those who control the drug trade, the extent to which property and sexual favours are exchanged for drugs, tension both staff and inmates; beatings, requests for transfers to protective custody, pressure on visiting family members to import drugs, younger and weaker inmates being converted to mules for transporting drugs. And the litany goes on.

In my opinion, given the nature and extent of the problem, the appellants can no more have a reasonable expectation of privacy with respect to random selection urine sampling than they could have in Conway in relation to the frisk search, the count and the wind.

However, Mr. Justice Collver elected to couch his judgment in terms of the *Charter* sections relied upon by the appellants. In my respectful opinion in the course of doing so he took all of the appropriate factors into account, touching upon the relevant evidence from time to time, and reached the right conclusion.

In the discussion section of his reasons Mr. Justice Collver set the stage by reference to the purposes and principles of the correctional system:

Determination of the validity of the random urine testing program first requires a consideration of the purpose of the correctional system and the principles established to guide it, as expressed in the introductory provisions of the Act, which read:

3. The purpose of the federal correctional system is to contribute to the maintenance of a just, peaceful and safe society, by:

(a) carrying out sentences imposed by courts through
the safe and humane custody and supervision of offenders; and
(b) assisting the rehabilitation of offenders and their
reintegration into the community as law-abiding citizens through the provision
of programs in penitentiaries and in the community. (my emphasis)

S. 4 of the Act then sets out guiding principles, and those pertinent to this discussion are:

(a) that the protection of society be the paramount consideration in the corrections process;

(d) that the Service use the least restrictive measures consistent with the protection of the public, staff members and offenders;

(e) that offenders retain the rights and privileges of all members of society, except those rights and privileges that are necessarily removed or restricted as a consequence of the sentence;

I think that it is significant that the impugned s. 63 of the regulations carries forward the themes of security and safety:

63. (1) For the purposes of paragraph 54(b) of the Act, the Service may establish a random selection urinalysis program for the purpose of ensuring the security of the penitentiary and the safety of persons by deterring the use of and trafficking in intoxicants in the penitentiary.

(2) A random selection urinalysis program shall provide for samples to be provided by inmates whose names have been chosen by random selection from among the names of the entire inmate population of the penitentiary.(emphasis added)

Argument before this Court and, judging by the trial judge's reasons, in the court below followed the sequence of s. 7 considerations first, followed by s. 8. In my opinion where, as here, the principal alleged *Charter* breach is unreasonable search or seizure it is s. 8 which falls to be considered first. If there were no search or seizure there could not be a complaint under either section. If, by the application of the tests found in *R.* v. *Kokesh* (1990) 61 C.C.C. (3d) 207 (S.C.C.), the search or seizure is held not to be unreasonable it would seem to follow as a matter of logic that a consequent diminution of the liberty or security of the complainant would not be contrary to the principles of fundamental justice. The last sentence in the earlier quote from Conway appears to substantiate that conclusion: "There being no reasonable expectation of privacy, s. 8 of the *Charter* is not called into play; nor is s. 7 implicated."

The three *Kokesh* tests to be applied to determine
whether a search is or is not unreasonable are:

1. Is the search authorized by law?

2. Is the law reasonable?

3. Is the manner of carrying out the search reasonable?

The first does not require comment. The Act and the regulations authorize the search.

As to the second, the court is called upon to strike "a delicate balance.
. .between societal interests and individual rights": *Nova
Scotia Pharmaceutical Society* v. *The Queen*
[1992] 2 S.C.R. 606 at 642; and *Cunningham* v.
*The Queen* (1993) 80 C.C.C. (3d) 492 (S.C.C.)
at 499. Both of those are s. 7 cases but the weighing process appears to be
the same as in s. 8. The trial judge understood the requirement for a balancing
exercise. He specifically referred to the submissions of counsel on the point
at p. 11 of his reasons. And at p. 15, after referring to the means by which
drugs are introduced into the institution he described what he called the "uneasy
choice" facing the prison authorities:

That presents CSC with an uneasy choice -- dissuading drug smugglers by intensifying interdiction efforts, or deterring importation by concentrating on inmate users. The former is unacceptable since it would necessarily entail intrusive body searches and x-rays. The latter has spawned this lawsuit.

Given the magnitude and pervasiveness of the problem and the minimal intrusion into the already limited privacy expectation of the inmates I have no difficulty in concluding that the balance falls heavily in favour of the societal interest. It follows that in my opinion the law is reasonable. In reaching that opinion I have taken into account the allegations about over breadth and unfairness associated with the random aspect, and about the absence of empirical data to prove that the objectives will be achieved.

The third *Kokesh* test requires consideration of the manner of carrying out the program. That subject is dealt with in part by s. 66 of the regulations and in part by Commissioner's Directive No. 572 and guidelines to the Directive, all emanating from the Correctional Service of Canada. Much was made in argument of the fact that neither the Directive nor the guidelines have the force of law but that seems to me to be irrelevant. Together the regulations, the Directive and the guidelines prescribe the manner of carrying out the program. Mr. Justice Collver found that the requirements were "meticulously followed at Kent Institution". The evidence supporting that conclusion, which is not disputed, seems to me to be a sufficient account of the manner of carrying out to form the basis for a finding as to reasonableness whether the Directive and the guidelines can or cannot properly be described as law.

Mr. Justice Collver summed up his view of the reasonable manner test as applied to the facts of this case in these words at p. 17 of his reasons:

In any event, the evidence establishes that seizures are carried out in a reasonable manner. Although urination on demand is unusual, the two hour time period given for compliance addresses that. Although urination in the presence of an observer (of the same sex) is also unusual, the need to ensure that the sample is not tampered with seems obvious, and the fact of observation does not, by itself, make the collection process unreasonable.

I agree with that conclusion. There is nothing useful which I can add to it.

As to s. 7 of the *Charter*, as I mentioned earlier, it seems to me that when a law is found to meet the *Kokesh* tests for purposes of a search or seizure under s. 8 it follows that the same law will meet the fundamental justice requirement of s. 7 to justify a deprivation or diminution of the right to liberty and security of the person. I take that proposition from the passage earlier quoted from *Conway*.

If, notwithstanding what I have said about the connection which appears to me to exist between ss. 7 and 8 of the *Charter* in search and seizure cases, s. 7 requires a kind of stand alone consideration I am content that my analysis of the s. 8 factors as are applied to the evidence in this case be applied also to s. 7. I am satisfied that the result would be the same. There may be one additional factor and that is a purpose test stated at p. 68 of *Rodriguez* in these words:

Where deprivation of the right in question does little or nothing to enhance the state's interest (whatever it may be), it seems to me that a breach of fundamental justice will be made out, as the individual's rights will have been deprived for no valid purpose.

It cannot, in my view, be seriously contended otherwise than that the prospect of advancing the state's interest in this case, given the nature of that interest, constitutes a valid purpose which justifies the infringement upon s. 7 rights.

The trial judge did not distinguish between s. 7 and s. 8 considerations in his discussion section or in his conclusions. As I have explained, I see no need to either. He ended his judgment with this summary paragraph:

Both fulfilment of its mandate and application of the statutory principles which guide its pursuit of that mandate justify the Correctional Service of Canada conducting random urine testing at Kent Institution. Moreover, as a deterrent to prison drug use and the violence associated with it, random urine testing constitutes neither an unreasonable diminution of inmates' liberty nor an unreasonable invasion of privacy or the integrity of their persons. I find no infringement of Sections 7 or 8 of the Charter.

I agree with that conclusion. Accordingly, I would dismiss the appeal.

GIBBS J.A.

**LAMBERT J.A.**:-- Mr. Justice Gibbs has expressed the views that underline his reservations about the process by which this petition was brought and has come before the Court. Notwithstanding those views and reservations Mr. Justice Gibbs has dealt with the substance of the petition and the appeal. I agree with Mr. Justice Gibbs' disposition of the substance of the appeal and I do so for the reasons he has given. I wish to express no views at all about the question about how this petition was framed or the propriety of bringing it before the Court in that way.

**HOLLINRAKE J.A.**:-- I agree with what both Mr. Justice Gibbs and Mr. Justice Lambert have said and I too would dismiss the appeal.

Statutes, Regulations and Rules Cited:

*Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms*, 1982, ss. 7, 8, 12, 15, 24.

*Constitution Act*, 1982, s. 52(1).

*Corrections and Conditional Release Act*, S.C. 1992, c. 20, ss. 2, 3, 4, 46, 54(b).

Corrections and Conditional Release Regulations SOR/92-620, ss. 60, 63, 66.

Counsel for the Appellants: S. Pawliuk and P. Benning.

Counsel for the Respondents: D. Fitzsimmons and C. Haskell.

The judgment of the Court was delivered by Gibbs J.A.

GIBBS J.A. (orally):-- In November of 1993 the appellants sued the respondents by writ of summons and statement of claim for a declaration that s. 54(b) of the *Corrections and Conditional Release Act* S.C. 1992, c. 20 and s. 60 and s. 63 of the Corrections and Conditional Release Regulations SOR/92-620 are of no force and effect because contrary to ss. 7, 8, 12, and 15 of the *Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms*. program, conducted without individualized grounds on a periodic basis and in

for; or
